2:24:1441:0:14:72:281:700:12:Times:12:g_d0_f1:Inferring Inequality with Home Production 
2:25:1482:14:14:72:264:686:12:Times:12:g_d0_f1:Job Boerma and Loukas Karabarbounis 
2:26:1517:14:14:72:236:672:12:Times:12:g_d0_f1:NBER Working Paper No. 24166 
2:27:1545:14:14:72:266:658:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:December 2017, Revised February 2019
2:28:1581:14:28:72:201:644:12:Times:12:Times:JEL No. D10,D60,E21,J22
2:29:1604:28:28:273:338:616:12:Times:12:Times:ABSTRACT
2:30:1612:28:14:72:546:588:12:Times:12:Times:We  revisit  the  causes,  welfare  consequences,  and  policy  implications  of  the  dispersion  in 
2:31:1714:14:14:72:545:574:12:Times:12:Times:households' labor  market  outcomes  using  a  model  with  uninsurable  risk,  incomplete  asset 
2:32:1811:14:14:72:546:560:12:Times:12:Times:markets,   and   home   production. Accounting   for   home   production   amplifies   welfare-based 
2:33:1911:14:14:72:546:546:12:Times:12:Times:differences   across   households   meaning   that inequality   is   larger   than   we   thought.   Home 
2:34:2016:14:14:72:546:532:12:Times:12:Times:production  does  not  offset  differences  that  originate  in the  market  sector  because  productivity 
2:35:2122:14:14:72:546:518:12:Times:12:Times:differences  in  the  home  sector  are  significant  and  the  time  input in  home  production  does  not 
2:36:2229:14:14:72:546:504:12:Times:12:Times:covary with consumption expenditures and wages in the cross section of households. The optimal 
2:37:2323:14:42:72:470:490:12:Times:12:Times:tax system should feature more progressivity taking into account home production.
2:38:2403:42:14:72:130:448:12:Times:12:Times:Job Boerma
2:39:2413:14:14:72:189:434:12:Times:12:Times:University of Minnesota
2:40:2436:14:14:72:198:420:12:Times:12:Times:Department of Economics
2:41:2459:14:14:72:132:406:12:Times:12:Times:Hanson Hall
2:42:2470:14:14:72:193:392:12:Times:12:Times:1925 Fourth Street South
2:43:2494:14:14:72:190:378:12:Times:12:Times:Minneapolis, MN 55455
2:44:2515:14:14:72:136:364:12:Times:12:Times:United States
2:45:2528:14:28:72:173:350:12:Times:12:Times:boerm002@umn.edu
2:46:2544:28:14:72:181:322:12:Times:12:Times:Loukas Karabarbounis
2:47:2564:14:14:72:189:308:12:Times:12:Times:University of Minnesota
2:48:2587:14:14:72:198:294:12:Times:12:Times:Department of Economics
2:49:2610:14:14:72:132:280:12:Times:12:Times:Hanson Hall
2:50:2621:14:14:72:190:266:12:Times:12:Times:Minneapolis, MN 55455
2:51:2642:14:14:72:124:252:12:Times:12:Times:and NBER
2:52:2650:14:0:72:156:238:12:Times:12:Times:loukas@umn.edu
3:53:2664:0:37:57:193:714:17:g_d0_f4:17:g_d0_f4:1    Introduction
3:54:2681:37:25:57:550:677:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A substantial body of research examines the causes, welfare consequences, and policy implications
3:55:2778:25:24:57:551:652:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of  the  pervasive  dispersion  across  households  in  their  labor  market  outcomes.1The  literature
3:56:2881:24:25:57:550:628:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:trying to understand the dispersion in wages, hours worked, and consumption expenditures across
3:57:2976:25:25:57:550:603:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:households typically abstracts from the possibility that households can produce goods and services
3:58:3074:25:25:57:550:578:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:outside of the market sector.  It is well known, however, that households spend roughly half as
3:59:3169:25:25:57:550:553:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:much  time  in  home  production  activities  such  as  child  care,  shopping,  and  cooking  as  in  the
3:60:3275:25:25:57:96:528:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:market.
3:61:3282:25:25:75:551:503:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:While it is understood that home production of goods and services introduces,  on average,
3:62:3372:25:25:57:550:478:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:a gap between households’ consumption as recorded in official statistics and their standards of
3:63:3467:25:25:57:550:453:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:living, little is known about how differences in home production across households affect inequality
3:64:3567:25:25:57:550:428:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in  standards  of  living.   A  priori  there  are  good  reasons  why  home  production  can  change  the
3:65:3673:25:25:57:550:403:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:inferences economists draw from observing dispersion in labor market outcomes.  To the extent
3:66:3766:25:24:57:550:378:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:that households are willing to substitute between market expenditures and time in the production
3:67:3862:24:25:57:550:354:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of goods and services, home production will tend to compress welfare differences that originate in
3:68:3960:25:25:57:550:329:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the market sector.  However, to the extent that household differences in the home sector remain
3:69:4055:25:25:57:550:304:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:uninsurable and are large relative to the market sector, the home sector itself may emerge as an
3:70:4151:25:25:57:351:279:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:additional source of welfare differences across households.
3:71:4210:25:25:75:551:254:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We show that incorporating home production in a model with uninsurable risk and incomplete
3:72:4300:25:25:57:550:229:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:asset markets changes the inferred sources of heterogeneity across households, alters meaningfully
3:73:4398:25:25:57:550:204:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the welfare consequences of dispersion, and leads to different policy conclusions.  Surprisingly, we
3:74:4498:25:25:57:550:179:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:infer that inequality across households is larger than what one would infer without incorporating
3:75:4595:25:18:57:550:154:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:home  production.2We  reach  this  conclusion  because,  for  households  of  all  ages,  productivity
3:76:4697:18:15:71:550:136:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:1See Heathcote,  Perri,  and Violante(2010) andAttanasio and Pistaferri(2016) for empirical regularities on
3:77:4804:15:8:57:279:121:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:household heterogeneity in labor market outcomes.
3:78:4853:8:16:71:550:113:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:2We use the term dispersion to refer to the variation in observed outcomes (such as time allocation, consumption
3:79:4965:16:12:57:550:97:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:expenditures, and wages) or inferred sources of heterogeneity (such as permanent or transitory productivity and
3:80:5076:12:12:57:550:85:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:taste  shifters).   We  use  the  term  inequality  to  refer  to  the  mapping  from  dispersion  to  measures  that  capture
3:81:5202:12:0:57:219:73:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:welfare differences across households.
4:82:5241:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:differences in the home sector are large and the time input in home production does not covary
4:83:5335:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:negatively with consumption expenditures and wages in the cross section of households.  Thus,
4:84:5428:25:25:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:home production does not offset differences that originate in the market sector.  Rather, home
4:85:5522:25:25:57:252:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:production amplifies these differences.
4:86:5561:25:24:75:551:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We develop our findings using a general equilibrium model with home production, heteroge-
4:87:5650:24:25:57:550:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:neous households that face idiosyncratic risk, and incomplete asset markets.  In the spirit ofGhez
4:88:5748:25:25:57:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and Becker(1975), households produce goods with a technology that uses as inputs both expendi-
4:89:5842:25:25:57:550:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:tures and time.  In the home sector, households are heterogeneous with respect to their disutility
4:90:5940:25:25:57:550:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of work in some activities and with respect to their productivity in other activities.  Home pro-
4:91:6037:25:25:57:550:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:duction is not tradeable and there are no assets that households can purchase to explicitly insure
4:92:6135:25:25:57:550:465:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:against differences that originate in the home sector.  In the market sector, households are also
4:93:6232:25:25:57:550:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:heterogeneous with respect to their disutility of work and their productivity.  The structure of
4:94:6328:25:25:57:550:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:asset markets allows households to insure against transitory shocks in their market productivity
4:95:6424:25:25:57:550:390:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:but not against permanent productivity differences.  We retain tractability and prove identifica-
4:96:6521:25:25:57:550:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:tion by extending the no-trade result with respect to certain assets for the one-sector model of
4:97:6617:25:24:57:550:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Heathcote, Storesletten, and Violante(2014) to our model embedding multiple sectors.  There-
4:98:6709:24:25:57:550:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:fore, we can characterize the allocations of time and consumption goods in closed form without
4:99:6803:25:25:57:314:291:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:simultaneously solving for the wealth distribution.
4:100:6854:25:25:75:551:266:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:At the core of our approach lies an observational equivalence theorem that allows us to compare
4:101:6949:25:25:57:550:241:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:our model with home production to a nested model without home production.  The observational
4:102:7041:25:25:57:550:216:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:equivalence theorem states that both models account perfectly for any given cross-sectional data
4:103:7137:25:25:57:550:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:on three observables:  consumption expenditures, time spent working in the market sector, and
4:104:7230:25:25:57:550:166:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:market productivity (wages).  However,  the inferred sources of heterogeneity that generate the
4:105:7325:25:25:57:550:141:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:data and inequality will in general differ between the two models.  It is essential for our purposes
4:106:7425:25:25:57:550:116:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:that the two models are observationally equivalent because any differences between the two models
4:107:7522:25:25:57:550:91:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:is exclusively driven by structural factors and not by their ability to account for cross-sectional
4:108:7621:25:0:57:550:66:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:data on labor market outcomes.  We first infer heterogeneity in market productivity and disutility
5:109:7720:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of work such that the allocations generated by the model without home production match the
5:110:7810:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:cross-sectional data on the three observables.  Then, we infer the sources of heterogeneity such
5:111:7906:25:25:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:that the allocations generated by the model with home production match the same cross-sectional
5:112:8001:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:data and, additionally, time spent on home activities that are subject to either productivity or
5:113:8097:25:24:57:169:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:preference differences.
5:114:8120:24:25:75:551:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:To investigate how incorporating home production changes our inferences, we apply our obser-
5:115:8212:25:25:57:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:vational equivalence theorem to U.S. data between 1995 and 2016.  We use data on consumption
5:116:8304:25:25:57:550:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:expenditures, time spent on the market sector, and market productivity from the Consumption
5:117:8395:25:25:57:550:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Expenditure Survey (CEX). The CEX does not contain information on time spent on home pro-
5:118:8484:25:25:57:550:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:duction.  To overcome this problem, we use data from the American Time Use Survey (ATUS)
5:119:8572:25:25:57:550:465:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:to  impute  individuals’  time  spent  on  home  production  based  on  observables  that  are  common
5:120:8674:25:25:57:550:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:between the two surveys.  We allow households to have different preferences over time uses such
5:121:8769:25:25:57:550:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:as cooking and cleaning because we find that these activities map closest to occupations that are
5:122:8866:25:25:57:550:390:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:intensive in manual skills.  By contrast, other time uses such as child care and nursing are less
5:123:8963:25:25:57:524:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:intensive in manual skills and we allow households to have different productivities in them.
5:124:9055:25:24:75:551:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The key result of our analysis is that the world is more unequal than we thought when we take
5:125:9148:24:25:57:550:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:into account home production.  We arrive at our conclusion using four ways to map dispersion in
5:126:9243:25:25:57:550:291:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:labor market outcomes into welfare-based measures of inequality.  First, the standard deviation
5:127:9338:25:25:57:550:266:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of equivalent variation across households is roughly 15 percent larger when we incorporate home
5:128:9433:25:25:57:550:241:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:production.   Second,  equalizing  marginal  utilities  across  households  requires  transfers  with  a
5:129:9537:25:25:57:550:216:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:standard deviation that is roughly 30 percent higher in the model with home production than
5:130:9628:25:25:57:550:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in the model without home production.  Third, an unborn household is willing to sacrifice up to
5:131:9723:25:25:57:550:166:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:13 percent of lifetime consumption in order to eliminate heterogeneity in an environment with
5:132:9816:25:25:57:550:141:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:home production, compared to 6 percent in an environment without home production.  Finally,
5:133:9907:25:25:57:550:116:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:taking into account home production, a utilitarian government would choose a more progressive
5:134:10000:25:25:57:550:91:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:tax  system.   For  example,  a  household  earning  200,000  dollars  would  face  an  average  tax  rate
5:135:10106:25:0:57:550:66:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of  21  percent  with  home  production,  compared  to  13  percent  without  home  production.   One
6:136:10208:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:way to understand our inequality result is in terms of the distinction between consumption and
6:137:10302:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:expenditures emphasized by Aguiar and Hurst(2005).  We find that market expenditures are less
6:138:10395:25:25:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:dispersed than the market value of total consumption which, in addition to market expenditures,
6:139:10490:25:25:57:370:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:includes the market value of time spent on home production.
6:140:10549:25:24:75:551:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Heterogeneity in home productivity rather than disutility of work is essential in amplifying
6:141:10641:24:25:57:550:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:inequality across households.  If there was only preference heterogeneity in the home sector, there
6:142:10740:25:25:57:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:would be no significant difference in inequality between the model with and the model without
6:143:10833:25:25:57:550:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:home  production.   Our  inference  of  home  productivity  is  based  on  an  intra-period  optimality
6:144:10936:25:25:57:550:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:condition which requires households to consume more in their more productive sector and implies
6:145:11031:25:25:57:550:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:a log-linear relationship between home productivity and three observables (market expenditures,
6:146:11126:25:25:57:550:465:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:time  spent  on  home  production,  and  market  productivity).   Home  productivity  cumulates  the
6:147:11226:25:25:57:550:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:variances of these three observables because the covariation between them is relatively small.  As
6:148:11324:25:25:57:487:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:a result, we find that significant productivity differences at home across households.
6:149:11410:25:25:75:551:390:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Our results are robust to a battery of sensitivity checks.  First, our conclusions are robust to
6:150:11506:25:25:57:550:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the estimated values of the elasticity of substitution across sectors, the parameter that governs
6:151:11603:25:24:57:550:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the Frisch elasticity of labor supply, and the progressivity of the tax system.  Second, our results
6:152:11703:24:25:57:550:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:apply separately within subgroups of households defined by their age, marital status, number of
6:153:11798:25:25:57:550:291:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:children, the presence of a working spouse, and education levels.  Third, our conclusions are robust
6:154:11898:25:25:57:550:266:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:to measures of expenditures that range from narrow (food) to broad (total spending including
6:155:11990:25:25:57:550:241:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:durables).  Fourth, our results are robust to even large amounts of measurement error in market
6:156:12085:25:25:57:550:216:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:expenditures,  market  hours,  and  home  hours.   Fifth,  we  examine  four  alternative  datasets  in
6:157:12188:25:25:57:550:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:which  we  do  not  need  to  impute  home  production  time  because  they  contain  information  on
6:158:12289:25:25:57:550:166:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:both expenditures and time use.  We confirm our results in the Panel Study of Income Dynamics
6:159:12382:25:25:57:550:141:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:(PSID) with food expenditures, in a version of the PSID with expanded consumption categories,
6:160:12475:25:25:57:387:116:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in a dataset from Japan, and in a dataset from the Netherlands.
6:161:12538:25:25:75:551:91:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:There is an extensive literature that examines how non-separabilities and home production
6:162:12627:25:0:57:550:66:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:affect  consumption  and  labor  supply  either  over  the  business  cycle  (Benhabib,  Rogerson,  and
7:163:12731:0:25:57:551:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Wright, 1991;Greenwood and Hercowitz,1991;McGrattan, Rogerson, and Wright,1997; Baxter
7:164:12817:25:25:57:551:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and Jermann,1999;Aguiar, Hurst, and Karabarbounis,2013) or over the life-cycle (Rios-Rull,
7:165:12907:25:25:57:551:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:1993;Aguiar and Hurst,2005,2007;Dotsey,  Li,  and Yang,2014).  In these papers,  home pro-
7:166:12997:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:duction provides a smoothing mechanism against differences that originate in the market sector
7:167:13091:25:24:57:550:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:if households are sufficiently willing to substitute expenditures with time.  Our conclusions for
7:168:13188:24:25:57:550:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the role of home production in understanding cross-sectional patterns differ from this literature
7:169:13285:25:25:57:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:because in the data we find that time in home production is not negatively correlated with wages
7:170:13381:25:25:57:550:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and consumption expenditures in the cross section of households.  By contrast,  an assumption
7:171:13474:25:25:57:550:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:underlying the business cycle and life-cycle literatures is that decreases in the opportunity cost
7:172:13572:25:25:57:550:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of time and in consumption expenditures are associated with substantial increases in time spent
7:173:13667:25:25:57:163:465:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:on home production.
7:174:13686:25:25:75:551:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Even though the home production literature has emphasized shocks in the home sector in order
7:175:13778:25:25:57:550:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:to generate higher volatility in labor markets and labor wedges (Benhabib, Rogerson, and Wright,
7:176:13874:25:25:57:550:390:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:1991;Greenwood and Hercowitz,1991;Karabarbounis,2014), little is known about cross-sectional
7:177:13966:25:25:57:550:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:differences in home productivity and tastes.  We develop a methodology to infer productivity and
7:178:14062:25:24:57:550:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:preference heterogeneity in the home sector.  Our conclusion is that these sources of heterogeneity
7:179:14161:24:25:57:550:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:are important in terms of generating cross-sectional and life-cycle patterns of expenditures and
7:180:14257:25:25:57:137:291:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:time allocation.
7:181:14273:25:25:75:551:266:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The literature on incomplete markets has started to incorporate home production and non-
7:182:14361:25:25:57:551:241:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:separabilities  into  models.Kaplan(2012)  argues  that  involuntary  unemployment  and  non-
7:183:14454:25:25:57:550:216:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:separable preferences allow an otherwise standard model with self-insurance to account for the
7:184:14548:25:25:57:551:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:variation  of  market  hours  over  the  life-cycle.Blundell,  Pistaferri,  and  Saporta-Eksten(2016)
7:185:14649:25:25:57:550:166:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:examine consumption inequality in a model in which shocks can also be insured within the family
7:186:14744:25:25:57:551:141:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and  preferences  for  hours  are  non-separable  across  spouses.Blundell,  Pistaferri,  and  Saporta-
7:187:14847:25:25:57:551:116:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Eksten(2018) incorporate child care into a life-cycle partial equilibrium model of consumption
7:188:14941:25:25:57:550:91:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and family labor supply.  Their paper aims to understand the responsiveness of consumption and
7:189:15035:25:0:57:550:66:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:time use to transitory and permanent wage shocks and, unlike our paper, it does not quantify
8:190:15128:0:25:57:340:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the extent to which home production affects inequality.
8:191:15183:25:25:75:551:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Another related literature addresses consumption inequality.  Earlier work (Deaton and Pax-
8:192:15274:25:25:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:son,  1994;  Gourinchas  and  Parker,  2002;  Storesletten,  Telmer,  and  Yaron,2004;Aguiar  and
8:193:15371:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Hurst,2013) has examined the drivers of life-cycle consumption inequality and their welfare con-
8:194:15467:25:24:57:550:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:sequences.   More  recent  work  focuses  on  the  increase  of  consumption  inequality  (Krueger  and
8:195:15570:24:25:57:551:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Perri,2006;Blundell,  Pistaferri,  and  Preston,2008;Aguiar  and  Bils,2015)  and  the  decline  in
8:196:15669:25:25:57:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:leisure inequality (Attanasio, Hurst, and Pistaferri,2014) over time.  Our contribution is to intro-
8:197:15769:25:25:57:550:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:duce home production data into the inequality literature and show that they change the inferences
8:198:15866:25:25:57:551:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:we draw about welfare.  Closest to the spirit of our exercise,Jones and Klenow(2016) map differ-
8:199:15962:25:25:57:550:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:ences in consumption levels and dispersion, market hours, and mortality into welfare differences
8:200:16058:25:25:57:524:465:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:across countries and find that in some cases GDP per capita does not track welfare closely.
8:201:16149:25:25:75:551:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Finally,  our  paper  relates  to  a  strand  of  literature  that  uses  no-trade  theorems  to  derive
8:202:16253:25:25:57:550:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:analytical  solutions  for  a  certain  class  of  models  with  incomplete  markets  and  heterogeneous
8:203:16357:25:25:57:550:390:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:agents.Constantinides  and  Duffie(1996)  first  derived  a  no-trade  theorem  in  an  endowment
8:204:16454:25:25:57:550:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:economy.Krebs(2003) extends the theorem to an environment with capital, in which households
8:205:16545:25:24:57:550:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:invest a constant share of their wealth in physical and human capital and their total income follows
8:206:16645:24:25:57:550:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:a random walk in logs.  Most relevant for us,Heathcote, Storesletten, and Violante(2014) extend
8:207:16740:25:25:57:550:291:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the no-trade theorem by allowing for partial insurance of wage shocks and flexible labor supply.
8:208:16836:25:25:57:550:266:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Our  contribution  is  to  extend  the  theorem  in  a  multi-sector  model  under  log  preferences  with
8:209:16942:25:25:57:550:241:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:respect to the consumption function that aggregates market and non-market inputs.  Importantly,
8:210:17037:25:25:57:550:216:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:with log preferences, we do not need to place restrictions on either the stochastic process governing
8:211:17138:25:40:57:473:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:home productivity and preferences or the elasticity of substitution across sectors.
8:212:17221:40:37:57:140:151:17:g_d0_f4:17:g_d0_f4:2    Model
8:213:17231:37:25:57:550:114:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We first present the model and characterize its equilibrium in closed form.  We then show how
8:214:17324:25:24:57:550:89:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:to infer the sources of heterogeneity across households such that the model accounts perfectly for
8:215:17422:24:0:57:471:65:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:cross-sectional data on consumption expenditures, allocation of time, and wages.
9:216:17503:0:33:57:185:714:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:2.1    Environment
9:217:17521:33:24:57:550:681:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f5:Demographics.   The  economy  features  perpetual  youth  demographics.   We  denote  bytthe
9:218:17613:24:25:57:550:657:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:calendar year and byjthe birth year of a household.  Households face a constant probability of
9:219:17707:25:25:57:551:632:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:survivalδin each period.  Each period a cohort of mass 1−δis born, keeping the population size
9:220:17801:25:37:57:204:607:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:constant with a mass of one.
9:221:17829:37:24:57:551:570:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f5:Goods and Time. Goods are produced in three sectors with labor. The vectorh= (hM,hN,hP)
9:222:17916:24:25:57:550:546:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:contains hours worked in each sector.  Households derive utility from consuming a bundle of three
9:223:18013:25:25:57:551:521:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:goodsc= (cM,cN,cP).  We denote bycMthe consumption of goods purchased in the market
9:224:18096:25:25:57:550:496:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:sector.  In the home sector, households consume two imperfectly substitutable goodscNandcP.
9:225:18187:25:25:57:550:471:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The key difference between the two home production sectors is that there is heterogeneity across
9:226:18283:25:25:57:551:446:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:households in how productively they transformhNintocNwhereas the production function of
9:227:18370:25:25:57:551:421:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:cPis identical across households.  To give some concrete examples from our quantitative results,
9:228:18466:25:25:57:550:396:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:we think of time spent on activities such as child care and nursing as belonging tohNbecause
9:229:18558:25:25:57:550:371:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:these activities are relatively less intensive in manual skills and productivity differences across
9:230:18657:25:25:57:550:346:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:households are likely to be a significant source of dispersion.  We think of time spent on activities
9:231:18758:25:25:57:551:321:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:such as cooking and cleaning as belonging tohPbecause these activities are more intensive in
9:232:18850:25:24:57:550:296:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:manual  skills  and  differences  in  preferences  are  more  likely  to  be  important  than  productivity
9:233:18957:24:37:57:113:272:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:differences.
9:234:18969:37:25:57:550:235:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f5:Technologies.  Households have access to a technology in the market sector and two technologies
9:235:19064:25:24:57:550:210:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in the home sector.  A household’s technology in the market sector is characterized by its (pre-
9:236:19160:24:25:57:551:186:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:tax) earningsy=zMhM, wherezMdenotes market productivity (wage) andhMdenotes hours
9:237:19241:25:0:57:397:161:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:worked  in  the  market  sector.   Aggregate  production  is  given  by
9:238:19312:0:14:403:409:170:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f12:∫
9:239:19313:14:20:408:550:156:9:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f9:ιzM(ι)hM(ι)dΦ(ι),  whereι
9:240:19338:20:25:57:550:136:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:identifies households and Φ denotes the cumulative distribution function of households.  Goods
9:241:19432:25:25:57:529:111:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and labor markets are perfectly competitive and the wage per efficiency unit of labor is one.
9:242:19525:25:25:75:550:86:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The government taxes labor income to finance (wasteful) public expendituresGof the market
9:243:19614:25:0:57:550:61:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f6:good.  Ify=zMhMis pre-tax earnings, then  ̃y= (1−τ0)z1−τ1MhMis after-tax earnings, whereτ0
10:244:19705:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:determines the level of taxes andτ1governs the progressivity of the tax system.  Whenτ1= 0
10:245:19795:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:there is a flat tax rate.  A higherτ1introduces a larger degree of progressivity into the tax system
10:246:19895:25:25:57:414:664:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f6:because it compresses after-tax earnings relative to pre-tax earnings.3
10:247:19966:25:25:75:551:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Production of home goods is given bycN=zNhNandcP=zPhP, wherezNandzPdenote
10:248:20039:25:24:57:551:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:home productivities.  We will allowzNto vary across households, whereas we fixzPto be the

10:250:20230:25:34:57:167:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:traded in the market.

10:293:22096:8:16:71:550:89:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:4Our model features a single decision maker within each household.  We model hours worked across spouses

14:398:29262:0:33:57:262:714:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:2.2    Equilibrium Allocations

14:401:29491:25:25:57:550:632:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:presents the proof.  Our proof follows very closely the proof presented in Heathcote, Storesletten,
14:402:29590:25:25:57:550:607:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and Violante (2014).  We extend their analysis along two dimensions.  First, we allow the disutility
14:403:29690:25:25:57:551:582:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of workBto be a random walk instead of a fixed effect.  Second, we extend the no-trade result
14:404:29783:25:25:57:265:557:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in an environment with multiple sectors.
14:405:29823:25:25:75:551:532:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We begin by guessing that the equilibrium features no trade across islands, that isx(ζjt+1;ι) =

14:407:30014:25:25:57:550:482:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:static planning problems.  The planner problems consist of maximizing average utility within each
14:408:30111:25:0:57:90:457:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:island,

14:424:30500:25:25:57:302:358:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:problems and all asset and goods markets clear.
14:425:30547:25:25:75:551:333:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We  obtain  the  no-trade  result  in  two  nested  versions  of  the  model.   The  first  model  sets
14:426:30650:25:25:57:551:308:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the preference weight on market consumption toωM= 1 and the preference weights on home
14:427:30736:25:25:57:551:283:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:consumption toωN=ωP= 0.  This is the environment without home production considered by
14:428:30822:25:25:57:550:258:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Heathcote, Storesletten, and Violante(2014).  The second model sets the curvature of utility with
14:429:30919:25:25:57:550:233:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:respect to consumption toγ=  1 for any value of (ωM,ωN,ωP).  The home production model
14:430:31005:25:25:57:550:208:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:nests the model without home production whenγ= 1, which is the case we consider below in

19:765:40265:11:20:416:449:458:9:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f5:exp(B).
19:766:40272:20:25:75:550:438:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Rows 1 to 6 show that, forγ= 1, the inferredα,ε, andBhave the same functional forms
19:767:40355:25:25:57:550:413:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:between the two models.  The difference is that the hours and consumption informative for the
19:768:40448:25:25:57:551:388:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:sources of heterogeneity in the home production model arehTin equation (10) andcTin equation
19:769:40540:25:25:57:550:363:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f9:(11),  whereas in the model without home productionhT=hMandcT=cM.  The inferredα
19:770:40620:25:25:57:549:338:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f11:depends positively on the consumption-hours ratio log(cT/hT) and market productivity logzM
19:771:40710:25:24:57:551:313:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and the inferredεis the difference between logzMandα.  The inferredBdepends on the gap
19:772:40796:24:25:57:460:289:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:between market productivity logzMand consumption logcTand hours loghT.
19:773:40866:25:25:75:551:264:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The new sources of heterogeneity in the home production model are presented in rows 7 and
19:774:40955:25:25:57:551:239:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:8.  These are inferred by substituting the production functionscP=zPhPandcN=zNhNinto
19:775:41039:25:25:57:341:214:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the first-order conditions (9) and solving forDandzN:
19:776:41092:25:17:66:104:189:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:exp(D)
19:777:41098:17:0:67:104:172:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:exp(B)

20:811:41832:30:15:57:550:562:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:Table  3  presents  an  example  with  parametersτ0=τ1=  0  andγ=η=  1.   The  upper  panel  shows  inference
20:812:41941:15:14:57:550:547:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:based on the model without home production and the lower panel shows inference based on the model with home
20:813:42048:14:15:57:551:533:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:production.  For the home production model we useωM=ωN=ωP= 1/3,zP= 20, andφ= 2.33.  The last
20:814:42140:15:33:57:529:518:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:column, labeledT, shows the equivalent variation for household 2 to achieve the utility level of household 1.
20:815:42249:33:25:75:551:485:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A numerical example in Table3 provides some insights for the mechanisms of the model and
20:816:42337:25:25:57:550:460:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:draws  lessons  from  the  observational  equivalence  theorem.   The  economy  is  populated  by  two
20:817:42439:25:25:57:550:435:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:households, there are no taxes, and preference parameters satisfyγ=η= 1.  In the upper panel,
20:818:42532:25:25:57:550:410:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the  economist  uses  the  model  without  home  production  to  infer  the  sources  of  heterogeneity.
20:819:42636:25:25:57:550:385:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Household  1  earns  a  wagezM=  20,  spendscM=  1,000,  and  workshM=  60.   Household
20:820:42723:25:25:57:550:360:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:2 also earnszM= 20,  but spendscM= 600 and workshM= 40.  The analytical solutions in
20:821:42807:25:25:57:551:335:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Table2 show that households with a higher expenditures to hours ratio,cM/hM, or higher market
20:822:42900:25:25:57:551:310:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:productivity,zM, have a higher uninsurable productivity componentα.  In Table3 we thus infer
20:823:42992:25:25:57:551:285:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:thatαis higher for household 1 than for household 2 (2.90 versus 2.85).  Since both households
20:824:43086:25:24:57:550:260:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:have the same market productivity andα+εadd up to (log) market productivity, we infer that
20:825:43176:24:25:57:551:236:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:household 2 has a higher insurable productivity componentεthan household 1.  Finally, we infer
20:826:43270:25:25:57:550:211:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:that household 2 has a higherBbecause it spends less and works less than household 1 despite
20:827:43362:25:25:57:249:186:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:having the same market productivity.
20:828:43398:25:25:75:551:161:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:In  the  lower  panel  the  economist  uses  the  home  production  model  to  infer  the  sources  of
20:829:43500:25:25:57:551:136:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:heterogeneity.  In addition to the same data on (cM,hM,zM), now the economist uses that the
20:830:43591:25:25:57:551:111:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:first household workshN= 10 andhP= 50 hours and the second household workshN= 50 and
20:831:43675:25:25:57:550:86:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:hP= 30 hours in the two sectors.  The inferredαnow depends on the ratio of the market value
20:832:43766:25:0:57:551:61:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of total consumption to total hours,cT/hT, rather than on the ratio of market expenditures to
21:833:43861:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:market hourscM/hM.  Since both households have the same market value of total consumption,
21:834:43951:25:25:57:551:689:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:cT=  2,200,  and  the  same  total  hours,hT=  120,  theα’s  are  equal.   Given  the  same  market
21:835:44050:25:25:57:551:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:productivity, theε’s are also equalized.  Given that the two households consume and work the
21:836:44142:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:same,  theB’s  are  also  equalized.   Equation  (12)  shows  thatDis  also  the  same  between  the
21:837:44242:25:24:57:550:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:two  households  because  they  have  the  same  ratio  of  home  production  to  market  expenditures
21:838:44344:24:25:57:550:590:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:zMhP/cM.   As  Table3  shows,  all  differences  in  observables  between  the  two  households  are
21:839:44444:25:25:57:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:loaded into home productivityzN.  We infer thatzNis higher for household 2 because it has a
21:840:44535:25:25:57:550:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:higher value of home production to market expenditureszMhN/cMand the sectors are substitutes
21:841:44627:25:25:57:99:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:(φ >1).
21:842:44634:25:25:75:551:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:There are two lessons we draw from this example.  First, home productivityzNis dispersed
21:843:44722:25:25:57:551:465:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:across households and absorbs dispersion one would attribute to (α,ε,B) in the absence of home
21:844:44816:25:25:57:550:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:production.  This result generalizes in our quantitative application using U.S. data below where
21:845:44912:25:25:57:550:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:we find thatzNis significantly more dispersed thanzMand that the dispersion in (α,ε,B) is
21:846:45001:25:25:57:256:390:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:smaller in the home production model.
21:847:45038:25:25:75:551:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The second lesson we draw concerns the welfare implications of labor market dispersion.  A
21:848:45128:25:24:57:550:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:household’s welfare ranking depends on whether the data has been generated by a model with
21:849:45218:24:25:57:550:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:or without home production.  The last column of Table3 shows equivalent variationsT, equal to
21:850:45311:25:25:57:550:291:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the transfers required for households to achieve a given level of utility if they re-optimize their
21:851:45410:25:25:57:550:266:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:consumption and hours choices.  The reference utility level in Table3 is the utility of household
21:852:45507:25:25:57:550:241:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:1 and, thus,Tfor household 1 is always equal to zero.  In the model without home production,
21:853:45599:25:25:57:550:216:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:Tfor household 2 equals 399.  In the home production model, the two households are identical in
21:854:45694:25:25:57:550:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:terms of their (α,ε,B,D), but household 2 has a higher home productivityzN.  Therefore, the
21:855:45785:25:34:57:289:166:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:welfare ranking changes andTbecomes -776.
21:856:45826:34:33:57:167:132:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:2.4    Discussion
21:857:45843:33:25:57:550:99:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Before  proceeding  to  the  quantitative  results,  we  pause  to  make  three  comments.   First,  we
21:858:45946:25:25:57:550:74:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:emphasize  the  importance  of  developing  an  equilibrium  model  that  expresses  the  arguments
21:859:46045:25:0:57:550:49:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:(cM,hM,hN,hP) of the utility function in terms of productivity and preference shifters and policy
22:860:46144:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:parameters.  An alternative approach, followed byKrueger and Perri(2003) in their study of the
22:861:46238:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:welfare effects of increasing inequality in the United States andJones and Klenow(2016) in their
22:862:46334:25:25:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:study of welfare and GDP differences across countries, is to plug what are endogenous variables
22:863:46429:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in our framework into the utility function and conduct welfare experiments by essentially varying
22:864:46526:25:24:57:550:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:these  variables.   While  our  approach  comes  with  additional  complexity,  it  has  the  conceptual
22:865:46630:24:25:57:550:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:advantage of taking into account equilibrium responses when conducting welfare analyses with
22:866:46722:25:25:57:437:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:respect to changes in more primitive sources of heterogeneity and policies.
22:867:46797:25:25:75:551:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Second, we wish to highlight the merits of theHeathcote, Storesletten, and Violante(2014)
22:868:46886:25:25:57:550:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:framework  used  in  our  analysis  compared  to  alternative  frameworks.   First,  standard  general
22:869:46988:25:25:57:551:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:equilibrium models with uninsurable risk, such asHuggett(1993) andAiyagari(1994) and ex-
22:870:47076:25:25:57:551:465:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:tensions with endogenous labor supply such asPijoan-Mas(2006),Chang and Kim(2007), and
22:871:47162:25:25:57:551:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Marcet, Obiols-Homs, and Weil(2007), feature self-insurance via a risk-free bond.  Solutions to
22:872:47257:25:25:57:550:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:these models are obtained computationally.  While the present model also allows households to
22:873:47350:25:25:57:550:390:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:trade  a  risk-free  bond  (by  settingx(ζjt)  =  1  for  all  statesζjt),  the  assumptions  on  asset  mar-
22:874:47459:25:25:57:550:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:kets, stochastic processes, and preferences allow us to derive a no-trade result and characterize
22:875:47556:25:24:57:550:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:equilibrium allocations in closed form.  Owing to the analytical results, a major advantage of the
22:876:47654:24:25:57:551:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Heathcote, Storesletten, and Violante(2014) framework is the transparency and generality of the
22:877:47749:25:25:57:132:291:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f6:identification.8
22:878:47765:25:25:75:551:266:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Third, our non-parametric approach to identifying the sources of heterogeneity is such that
22:879:47856:25:25:57:550:241:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the model accounts perfectly for any given cross-sectional data on market consumption, hours,
22:880:47949:25:25:57:550:216:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and wages.  Conceptually, our approach is similar toHsieh and Klenow(2009) who infer wedges in
22:881:48043:25:25:57:550:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:first-order conditions such that firm-level outcomes generated by their model match data analogs.
22:882:48140:25:25:57:551:166:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Heathcote, Storesletten, and Violante(2014) also do not impose distributional assumptions on
22:883:48232:25:18:57:550:141:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the  sources  of  heterogeneity  in  order  to  estimate  model  parameters  and  parameters  of  these
22:884:48335:18:15:71:550:123:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:8Despite the wealth distribution not being an object of interest within this framework, a dynamic structure with
22:885:48447:15:12:57:550:108:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:non-labor income is still essential.  In a framework without non-labor income, households would maximize utility
22:886:48559:12:12:57:551:96:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:subject to home production technologiescN=zNhNandcP=zPhPand the budget constraintcM=zMhM.
22:887:48648:12:12:57:550:84:10:g_d0_f8:7:g_d0_f22:Here,  observed market productivityzMis constrained to equal the market consumption to hours ratiocM/hM
22:888:48751:12:0:57:447:72:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:and any choice of (zM,B,D,zN) is not sufficient to match data on (zM,cM,hN,hP,hM).
23:889:48835:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:distributions.  A difference withHeathcote, Storesletten, and Violante(2014) is that these authors
23:890:48933:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:select moments in order to estimate parameters using the method of moments.  Our approach,
23:891:49023:25:25:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:instead, does not require restrictions on which moments are more informative for the identification
23:892:49122:25:40:57:215:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of the sources of heterogeneity.
23:893:49154:40:36:57:261:599:17:g_d0_f4:17:g_d0_f4:3    Quantitative Results
23:894:49179:36:25:57:550:563:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We begin by describing the data sources and the parameterization of the model.  We then present
23:895:49274:25:35:57:340:538:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the inferred sources of heterogeneity across households.
23:896:49330:35:33:57:187:503:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:3.1    Data Sources
23:897:49349:33:24:57:550:470:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:For the baseline analyses we use data from the Consumer Expenditure Survey (CEX) and the
23:898:49437:24:25:57:550:446:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:American Time Use Survey (ATUS). We consider married and cohabiting households with heads
23:899:49526:25:25:57:550:421:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:between 25 and 65 years old who are not students.  We drop observations for households with a
23:900:49619:25:25:57:550:396:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:market productivity below 3 dollars or above 300 dollars per hour in 2010 dollars, with expen-
23:901:49713:25:25:57:550:371:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:ditures at the top and bottom one percent, and with respondents who indicated working more
23:902:49803:25:25:57:550:346:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:than 92 hours in the market or at home.  In the ATUS we drop respondents during weekends and
23:903:49895:25:25:57:550:321:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in the CEX we keep only households that completed all four interviews.  The final sample from
23:904:49988:25:25:57:550:296:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:CEX/ATUS  contains  32,993  households  between  1995  and  2016.   In  all  our  results,  we  weight
23:905:50090:25:25:57:369:271:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:households with the sample weights provided by the surveys.
23:906:50149:25:25:75:551:246:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Data for market expenditurescM, market productivityzM, and market hourshMcome from
23:907:50231:25:25:57:551:221:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:CEX  interview  surveys  collected  between  1996  and  2017.   Closest  to  the  definition  ofAguiar
23:908:50333:25:24:57:550:196:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and Hurst(2013), for our baseline analysescMis annual non-durable consumption expenditures
23:909:50423:24:25:57:550:172:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:which include food and beverages, tobacco, personal care, apparel, utilities, household operations
23:910:50521:25:25:57:550:147:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:(including child care), public transportation, gasoline, reading material, and personal care.  Non-
23:911:50620:25:25:57:550:122:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:durable  consumption  expenditures  exclude  health  and  education.   We  adjust  consumption  for
23:912:50719:25:25:57:221:97:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:household composition and size.
23:913:50750:25:0:75:551:72:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Our measure of income is the amount of wage and salary income before deductions earned
24:914:50838:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:over the past 12 months.  Individual wages are defined as income divided by hours usually worked
24:915:50934:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in a year, which is the product of weeks worked with usual hours worked per week.  We define
24:916:51026:25:25:57:549:664:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f11:household market hourshMas the sum of hours worked by spouses and market productivityzM
24:917:51113:25:25:57:462:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:as the average of wages of individual members weighted by their market hours.
24:918:51190:25:24:75:550:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Data for home hourshNandhPcome from the ATUS waves between 2003 and 2017.  Ran-
24:919:51269:24:25:57:550:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:domly selected individuals from a group of households that completed their eight and final month
24:920:51365:25:25:57:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:interview for the Current Population Survey report their activities on a 24-hour time diary of the
24:921:51463:25:25:57:550:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:previous day.  Similar to Aguiar, Hurst, and Karabarbounis (2013), total time spent on home pro-
24:922:51559:25:25:57:551:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:duction,hN+hP, includes housework, cooking, shopping, home and car maintenance, gardening,
24:923:51649:25:25:57:313:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:child care, and care for other household members.
24:924:51698:25:25:75:551:465:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Before using the ATUS data in our analyses, we need to separate total home production time
24:925:51788:25:25:57:551:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:betweenhNandhP.  Our approach is to map disaggregated time uses into occupations and then
24:926:51877:25:25:57:551:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:classify  inhNall  the  time  uses  mapped  into  occupations  that  perform  tasks  with  low  manual
24:927:51979:25:25:57:551:390:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:content and inhPall time uses mapped into occupations that perform tasks with high manual
24:928:52068:25:25:57:550:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:content.   The  logic  underlying  our  approach  is  that  time  activities  that  use  the  same  skills  as
24:929:52178:25:24:57:550:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:occupations with high manual content are less likely to display significant heterogeneity in terms
24:930:52276:24:25:57:550:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of productivity.  We use the mapping from time uses to occupations together with Occupational
24:931:52369:25:25:57:550:291:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Information Network (O*NET) task measures for various activities as described in the appendix
24:932:52462:25:25:57:551:266:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of Acemoglu and Autor(2011) to create an index of manual content for each disaggregated time
24:933:52554:25:25:57:550:241:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:use.9We  classify  activities  inhNif  they  have  a  manual  index  below  the  median  and  classify
24:934:52656:25:25:57:344:216:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:activities inhPif they have an index above the median.
24:935:52710:25:25:75:551:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The CEX does not contain information on time spent on home production.  To overcome this
24:936:52798:25:18:57:550:166:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:difficulty,  we  impute  time  use  data  from  the  ATUS  into  the  CEX.10Our  imputation  is  based
24:937:52900:18:15:71:550:148:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:9Because  there  are  many  such  indices,  we  standardize  the  task  measures  to  have  mean  zero  and  standard
24:938:53017:15:12:57:550:133:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:deviation of one and take the average across all manual tasks to create a single manual index.  We list the mapping
24:939:53132:12:12:57:550:121:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:for the seven largest time use categories.  Child care time is mapped to preschool teachers and child care workers;
24:940:53247:12:12:57:550:109:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:shopping time is mapped to cashiers; nursing time is mapped to registered nurses and nursing assistants; cooking
24:941:53359:12:12:57:550:97:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:is  mapped  to  food  preparation  and  serving  workers;  cleaning  is  mapped  to  maids  and  housekeeping  cleaners;
24:942:53479:12:12:57:550:85:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:gardening is mapped to landscaping and groundskeeping workers; laundry is mapped to laundry and dry-cleaning
24:943:53587:12:8:57:93:73:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:workers.
24:944:53595:8:0:67:551:65:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:10In  an  approach  similar  to  ours,  Blundell,  Pistaferri,  and  Saporta-Eksten(2018)  use  the  CEX  to  impute
25:945:53713:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:on an iterative procedure where individuals in the CEX are allocated the mean home hours (for
25:946:53806:25:25:57:551:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:each ofhNandhP) of matched individuals from the ATUS based on group characteristics.  We
25:947:53894:25:25:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:begin the procedure by matching individuals based on work status, race, gender, and age.  We
25:948:53986:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:then proceed to improve these estimates by adding a host of additional characteristics, such as
25:949:54081:25:24:57:550:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:family status,  education,  disability status,  geography,  hours worked,  and wages,  and matching
25:950:54180:24:25:57:550:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:individuals  based  on  these  characteristics  whenever  possible.   We  first  impute  home  hours  to
25:951:54284:25:25:57:525:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:individuals and, similarly to market hours, then sum up these hours at the household level.
25:952:54375:25:25:75:549:540:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f11:Our  imputation  accounts  for  approximately  two-thirds  of  the  variation  in  home  hourshN
25:953:54471:25:25:57:551:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:andhP.   In  Appendix  Table  A.1  we  confirm  that  our  imputation  does  not  introduce  spurious
25:954:54572:25:25:57:550:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:correlations in the merged CEX/ATUS data by showing that the correlation of home hours with
25:955:54663:25:25:57:550:465:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:market hours and wages conditional on age is similar in magnitude between the ATUS sample
25:956:54752:25:25:57:551:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of individuals and the merged CEX/ATUS sample of households.  In Appendix TablesA.2and
25:957:54838:25:25:57:551:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:A.3we show that, conditional on age, married men, women, less educated, and more educated
25:958:54927:25:25:57:550:390:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:exhibit similar correlations between wages, market hours, and home hours in the ATUS. Further,
25:959:55021:25:25:57:551:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in Appendix TablesA.4andA.5we show that the correlation of total home hours with market
25:960:55108:25:24:57:550:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:expenditures, market hours, and wages conditional on age is similar in magnitude between the
25:961:55200:24:25:57:550:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:CEX/ATUS sample of households and two PSID samples of households that do not require an
25:962:55287:25:25:57:550:291:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:imputation since they contain information on home hours, market expenditures, market hours,
25:963:55378:25:25:57:113:266:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and wages.
25:964:55388:25:25:75:550:241:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Table4  presents  summary  statistics  of  the  time  allocation  of  married  households  in  the
25:965:55486:25:25:57:550:216:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:CEX/ATUS sample along with the value of the manual index and the average wage corresponding
25:966:55577:25:25:57:551:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:to the occupations the time uses are mapped to.  Beginning with market hourshM, we note in the

27:1018:58439:30:15:57:551:474:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:Table 5 presents parameter values for the models without home production (ωM= 1) and with home production
27:1019:58544:15:34:57:102:459:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:(ωM<1).
27:1020:58551:34:25:57:550:425:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:to match an average tax rate on labor income equal to 0.10, which equals the average ratio of
27:1021:58644:25:25:57:475:400:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:personal current taxes to income from the national income and product accounts.
27:1022:58723:25:25:75:551:375:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:For the home production model, we obtained the equilibrium allocations in closed form only
27:1023:58813:25:25:57:551:350:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:under a curvature of the utility function with respect to consumption equal toγ= 1.  We choose
27:1024:58907:25:25:57:550:325:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:γ= 1 also for model without home production.  Is is essential to nest the model without home
27:1025:58999:25:25:57:550:300:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:production, so that welfare differences across the two models do not arise from different curvatures
27:1026:59099:25:24:57:330:275:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f6:of the utility function with respect to consumption.13
27:1027:59153:24:25:75:551:251:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Next, we estimate the parameterηfor the curvature of the utility function with respect to
27:1028:59242:25:25:57:549:226:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f11:hours.  Our strategy is to chooseηin each model such that a regression of log market hours loghM
27:1029:59338:25:25:57:551:201:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:on the transitory component of market productivityεyields a coefficient of 0.54.  The target value
27:1030:59436:25:25:57:550:176:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of 0.54 comes from the meta analysis of estimates of the intensive margin Frisch elasticity from
27:1031:59532:25:22:57:550:151:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:micro variation found inChetty,  Guren,  Manoli,  and Weber(2012).  Consistent with the logic
27:1032:59625:22:12:57:550:129:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:subsuming these transfers.  Our estimated tax parameter is close to the estimate of 0.19 in Heathcote, Storesletten,
27:1033:59741:12:12:57:551:117:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:and Violante (2014).  Using their tax function log  ̃y= constant + (1−τ1) logy, we would estimateτ1= 0.15.  We,
27:1034:59852:12:12:57:550:105:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:therefore,  argue  that  it  is  relatively  inconsequential  whether  we  apply  the  progressivity  parameter  (1−τ1)  to
27:1035:59975:12:8:57:239:93:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:after-tax wages or after-tax labor income.
27:1036:60017:8:16:67:550:85:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f16:13While welfare effects are sensitive to the value ofγin the model without home production, our inference ofα
27:1037:60126:16:0:57:263:69:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:andεdoes not depend onγas seen in Table2.
28:1038:60169:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of Rupert, Rogerson, and Wright(2000) who argue that estimates of the Frisch elasticities are
28:1039:60262:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:downward biased in the presence of home production, we estimateη= 0.90 in the model without

28:1041:60416:25:25:75:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We  now  describe  the  estimation  of  the  preference  weights  (ωM,ωN,ωP)  and  the  elasticity

28:1073:60765:41:25:57:551:502:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We use these two equations and the normalizationωM+ωN+ωP= 1 to solve for the weights for
28:1074:60853:25:25:57:551:477:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:any given value ofφ.  To do so, we require some normalization of the levels of productivity and
28:1075:60948:25:25:57:551:452:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:disutility of work.  From the estimates of wages in occupations matched with time uses in Table4
28:1076:61044:25:0:57:366:427:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f15:we setEzN= 0.58EzMandzP= 0.47EzM.  We normalizeE

28:1082:61112:25:24:75:550:402:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:For the elasticity of substitutionφ,  we again use the first-order condition (9) to derive the
28:1083:61206:24:35:57:110:378:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:regression:
28:1084:61217:35:0:65:80:343:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:log
28:1085:61220:0:9:82:91:360:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f12:(

28:1095:61280:35:25:57:550:308:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We note that estimation ofφusing data oncM/hNandzMwould lead to biased estimates if
28:1096:61363:25:24:57:551:283:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:zMandzNare  correlated.   For  this  reason,  we  take  changes  over  time  in  equation  (14)  and
28:1097:61463:24:25:57:549:259:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f11:use  a  synthetic  panel  approach  to  estimateφbased  on  changes  incM/hNand  changes  inzM
28:1098:61557:25:25:57:550:234:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:between the beginning and the end of the life-cycle.  The identifying assumption is that changes
28:1099:61653:25:17:57:551:209:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:inzNare uncorrelated with changes inzMbetween the beginning and the end of the life-cycle.
28:1100:61743:17:16:67:550:192:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:14Our strategy is conservative in the sense that the inequality difference between the two models becomes larger
28:1101:61855:16:12:57:550:176:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:when we setηto be equal between the two models.  The Frisch elasticity for effective total hourshTis (1−τ1)ηin
28:1102:61965:12:12:57:551:164:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:both models.  There are three reasons whyηdeviates from the targeted elasticity of 0.54.  First, the progressivity
28:1103:62079:12:12:57:550:152:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:of the tax system introduces the wedge 1−τ1betweenηand the Frisch elasticity for total hourshT.  Second, tastes
28:1104:62190:12:12:57:550:140:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:and home productivity are correlated with market wages.  Third, even without such a correlation, the elasticities
28:1105:62303:12:12:57:550:128:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:of market hourshMdiffer between the two models becausehM=hTwithout home production whereas with home
28:1106:62403:12:12:57:551:116:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:productionhMis negatively correlated tohNandhP.  In Appendix Table A.6 we present the various labor supply
28:1107:62509:12:8:57:223:104:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:elasticities implied by the two models.
28:1108:62548:8:17:67:550:96:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:15We have confirmed that these normalizations are inconsequential for all our results except for the levels of
28:1109:62658:17:15:57:551:79:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:productivity in the home sector.  This is because the productsωNz(φ−1)/φNandωPz(φ−1)/φPenter the utility function
28:1110:62771:15:0:57:328:64:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f15:and, therefore, for any given targeted value ofEzN,zP, andE
28:1111:62830:0:7:330:336:75:10:g_d0_f12:10:g_d0_f12:(
28:1112:62831:7:8:337:361:68:7:g_d0_f7:7:g_d0_f7:exp(B)
28:1113:62837:8:0:337:362:60:7:g_d0_f7:7:g_d0_f7:exp(D)
28:1114:62843:0:11:363:369:75:10:g_d0_f12:10:g_d0_f12:)
28:1115:62844:11:15:369:551:64:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:, the parameters (ωM,ωN,ωP) will adjust
28:1116:62883:15:0:57:275:49:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:so that the model matches exactly the same data.
29:1117:62933:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:This assumption is consistent with the assumptions underlying the no-trade result which requires
29:1118:63029:25:25:57:551:689:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:zN,t+1to be independent of innovations tozM,t+1.  Both our estimation strategy and the no-trade
29:1119:63124:25:25:57:469:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:theorem are consistent with a correlation of productivity across sectors in levels.
29:1120:63207:25:25:75:551:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We  estimate  that  market  and  home  goods  are  substitutes  with  an  elasticity  ofφ=  2.33.
29:1121:63304:25:24:57:550:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Our estimate of the elasticity of substitution is consistent with those found in the literature.  For
29:1122:63405:24:25:57:551:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:example, most estimates ofRupert, Rogerson, and Wright(1995) for couples fall between roughly
29:1123:63498:25:35:57:403:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:2 and 4 andAguiar and Hurst(2007) obtain estimates of around 2.
29:1124:63561:35:33:57:332:530:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:3.3    Inferred Sources of Heterogeneity
29:1125:63601:33:25:57:550:497:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We extract the sources of heterogeneity by plugging the estimated parameters and CEX/ATUS
29:1126:63690:25:24:57:551:472:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:data on (cM,hM,zM,hN,hP) into the solutions in Table2 for each household.  In Figure1 we
29:1127:63778:24:25:57:551:448:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:present the age profiles for the means of the productivity and taste shifters (α,ε,B,D,logzN).
29:1128:63872:25:25:57:550:423:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:To obtain these age profiles, we regress each inferred shifter on age dummies, cohort dummies,
29:1129:63966:25:25:57:551:398:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and normalized year dummies as inDeaton(1997).16We plot the coefficients on age dummies
29:1130:64053:25:25:57:550:373:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:which give the mean of each shifter by age relative to age 25.  To reduce noise in the figures, we
29:1131:64151:25:25:57:550:348:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:present the fitted values from locally weighted regressions of the coefficients on age dummies on
29:1132:64248:25:25:57:77:323:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:age.
29:1133:64252:25:25:75:550:298:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Recall from the analytical solutions in Table2, that the permanent component of productivity
29:1134:64344:25:25:57:551:273:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:αgrows over the life-cycle when either the ratio of consumption to hourscT/hTgrows or when
29:1135:64434:25:25:57:550:248:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:wageszMgrow.  The transitory componentεfalls when the increase incT/hTis large relative to
29:1136:64524:25:25:57:551:223:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the increase inzM.  In the upper panels of Figure1 we see that the means ofαandεare similar
29:1137:64615:25:24:57:551:198:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:until roughly age 45 between the two models, but diverge after that.  The slower growth ofαand
29:1138:64709:24:25:57:551:174:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the smaller decline inεin the model with home production reflect the significant decline in home
29:1139:64805:25:25:57:550:149:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:hourshNin the second part of the life-cycle which implies thatcT/hTgrows by less thancM/hM.
29:1140:64896:25:25:57:550:124:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:In the lower panels we see that both models generate a relatively similar increase in the disutility
29:1141:64996:25:18:57:516:99:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of workBwhich reflects the faster growth ofzMrelative tocTandhTover the life-cycle.

31:1192:67318:25:25:75:551:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:In Figure2 we present the age profiles for the cross-sectional variances of (α,ε,B,D,logzN),

31:1194:67506:25:25:57:550:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:dummies,  cohort  dummies,  and  normalized  year  dummies.   The  main  result  is  that  the  home
31:1195:67606:25:24:57:550:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:production  model  requires  significantly  smaller  variances  ofα,ε,  andBrelative  to  the  model
31:1196:67706:24:25:57:551:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:without home production.  From the solutions in Table2, we observe that the increasing variance
31:1197:67801:25:25:57:550:291:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:ofαover the life-cycle is driven by the increase in the variance of the consumption-hours ratio
31:1198:67896:25:25:57:551:266:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:log(cT/hT) and the increase in the variance of wages logzM.17Because the variance of log(cT/hT)
31:1199:67991:25:25:57:550:241:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:is lower than the variance of log(cM/hM), the home production model generates a lower variance
31:1200:68085:25:25:57:550:216:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:ofα.   Given  that  both  models  match  the  same  variance  of  logzMbut  the  home  production
31:1201:68182:25:25:57:550:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:model displays a larger covariance betweenαandεthan the model without home production
31:1202:68267:25:25:57:550:166:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:(see Appendix TableA.7),εturns out to be less dispersed in the home production model.  The
31:1203:68357:25:25:57:550:141:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:variance ofBis also smaller in the home production model which reflects the smaller variance of
31:1204:68452:25:18:57:451:116:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:a combination of logcTand loghTthan a combination of logcMand loghM.
31:1205:68520:18:15:67:550:98:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:17To derive analytical solutions, we have not allowed for borrowing constraints that are important when thinking
31:1206:68632:15:12:57:550:83:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:about the comovement of income with consumption at the bottom of the asset distribution.  While the transmission
31:1207:68744:12:12:57:550:71:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:mechanism is different than in our model, the presence of borrowing constraints generates comovement between
31:1208:68852:12:0:57:264:59:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:income and consumption in a similar way asα.
32:1209:68898:0:25:75:551:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:As is seen in the lower panels,  a fraction of the dispersion in observables is now accounted

32:1212:69177:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:is 0.33 in the data.  What explains the almost four times as large dispersion in logzN?  From

32:1220:69375:37:25:57:550:541:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Our result that home productivity is more dispersed than market productivity reflects the fact
32:1221:69469:25:25:57:550:516:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:that logzNcumulates the dispersions of three observables, log  ̃zM, loghN, and logcM, that are

32:1228:70128:25:24:57:551:341:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of log  ̃zM.  As equation (15) shows,  aroundφ= 1,  the variance of logzNtends to infinity.  To

33:1267:72944:40:36:57:366:230:17:g_d0_f4:17:g_d0_f4:4    Inequality and Home Production
33:1268:72979:36:25:57:550:194:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We begin by demonstrating that home production amplifies inequality across households.  Next,
33:1269:73072:25:25:57:550:169:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:we argue that heterogeneity in home productivity rather than preferences is crucial in amplifying
33:1270:73169:25:35:57:110:144:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:inequality.
33:1271:73180:35:33:57:368:109:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:4.1    Home Production Amplifies Inequality
33:1272:73223:33:24:57:550:76:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We demonstrate that inequality across households is larger in the home production model than
33:1273:73315:24:0:57:550:52:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in the model without home production, despite both models generating the same data on market
34:1274:73409:0:25:57:551:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:observables.20By inequality, we mean a mapping from the dispersion in observed allocations and
34:1275:73503:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:inferred sources of heterogeneity to measures that capture welfare differences across households.
34:1276:73600:25:31:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We acknowledge there are various such mappings and, therefore, present four inequality metrics.
34:1277:73695:31:33:57:219:633:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:4.1.1    Equivalent Variation

34:1281:74021:25:25:57:551:526:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:a value functionˆVt(ˆι).  For every householdι, we compute the income transferTt(ι) that makes

36:1361:79294:31:32:57:241:247:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:4.1.2    Redistributive Transfers
36:1362:79327:32:25:57:550:215:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Our second measure of inequality is the cross-sectional dispersion in redistributive transfers that
36:1363:79426:25:25:57:550:190:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:would equalize marginal utilities.  After households choose their allocations of consumption and
36:1364:79522:25:25:57:550:165:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:hours, we allow a utilitarian planner to redistribute aggregate market consumption across house-
36:1365:79618:25:25:57:550:140:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:holds in order to maximize the average of households’ utilities.  The dispersion in these transfers
36:1366:79717:25:25:57:550:115:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:captures the extent of redistribution required in order to maximize social welfare or, equivalently,
36:1367:79817:25:0:57:550:90:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:to equalize marginal utilities of market consumption.  Formally,  the problem we consider is to

38:1403:81514:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the life-cycle in the model with home production.  We obtain a similar result in the right panel

38:1405:81706:25:25:75:551:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:It is instructive to compare our findings using the market value of total consumptioncT(ι) to
38:1406:81799:25:25:57:551:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:other findings in the literature.  Some authors such asFrazis and Stewart(2011) andBridgman,
38:1407:81891:25:24:57:551:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Dugan, Lal, Osborne, and Villones(2012) have embraced the view that home production decreases
38:1408:81984:24:25:57:550:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:inequality.  Their argument is that, since home hours do not correlate with income in the cross
38:1409:82079:25:25:57:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:section of households, adding a constant value of home production across households results in
38:1410:82173:25:25:57:551:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:a smaller dispersion of total income.  Inspection of equation (11) forcTreveals a fundamental
38:1411:82266:25:25:57:550:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:difference  in  our  logic.   Home  hours  in  our  model  are  valued  at  their  opportunity  cost,  which
38:1412:82374:25:25:57:550:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:depends on market productivity and sectoral disutilities of work, and the opportunity cost varies
38:1413:82471:25:25:57:550:465:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:across households. Using a constant cost across households to value their home hours does not take
38:1414:82569:25:31:57:550:440:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f6:into account differences in the productivity of home hours or preferences for home production.24
38:1415:82665:31:33:57:330:409:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:4.1.3    Lifetime Welfare Cost of Heterogeneity
38:1416:82712:33:25:57:550:376:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:In this section we present the lifetime welfare effect arising from heterogeneity across households.
38:1417:82812:25:25:57:550:351:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:These  calculations  contrast  with  our  inequality  metrics  thus  far  which  have  ignored  dynamic
38:1418:82915:25:24:57:550:326:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:considerations.  Specifically, we calculate the fraction of lifetime consumption that a household
38:1419:83012:24:25:57:550:302:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:is willing to sacrifice ex-ante to be indifferent between being born in the baseline environment
38:1420:83108:25:25:57:550:277:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:with heterogeneity and allocations{ct,ht}and a counterfactual environment in which dimensions
38:1421:83201:25:25:57:550:252:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of heterogeneity are shut down.  The allocations in the counterfactual economy are denoted by
38:1422:83294:25:25:57:551:227:12:g_d0_f10:12:g_d0_f5:{ˆct,ˆht}and are generated using the equations in Table1 after shutting down particular dimensions
38:1423:83392:25:18:57:150:202:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f6:of heterogeneity.25
38:1424:83411:18:15:67:550:184:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:24A  reasonable  concern  with  using  wages  to  value  home  hours  is  that  some  households  or  members  of  the
38:1425:83529:15:12:57:550:169:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:household may be at a corner solution.  In practice, we are not concerned that this biases our results for three
38:1426:83641:12:12:57:550:157:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:reasons.  First, in our baseline CEX/ATUS sample of married households the fraction of households with either
38:1427:83750:12:12:57:550:145:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:zero market hours or zero home hours per year is less than one percent.  Further, sensitivity analyses presented
38:1428:83862:12:12:57:550:133:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:in  Section  5  confirm  our  inequality  results  for  a  sample  of  singles  and  for  a  subsample  of  married  households
38:1429:83989:12:12:57:550:121:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:with a working spouse for which valuation at market wages is less concerning.  Finally, our notion of inequality
38:1430:84101:12:12:57:551:109:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:in consumption allows for a wedge between the market wages and marginal value of home hourshParising from
38:1431:84206:12:9:57:215:97:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:preference differences across sectors.
38:1432:84244:9:15:67:551:88:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:25For  the  counterfactuals  in  this  section,  and  consistently  with  our  definition  of  equilibrium  in  whichGis
38:1433:84364:15:12:57:551:73:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:an  endogenous  variable,  we  choose  to  keep  constant  the  tax  parameters  (τ0,τ1)  because  we  prefer  to  evaluate
38:1434:84487:12:0:57:550:61:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:more direct welfare effects from heterogeneity rather than more nuanced effects arising from changes in the tax

39:1477:85990:25:25:57:398:190:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:tivity becausehTis negatively correlated withzMin both models.
