39:1479:86146:25:25:57:551:140:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:to a representative household economy.  The welfare cost of heterogeneityλis almost 13 percent in
39:1480:86243:25:24:57:550:115:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the model with home production as opposed to 6.5 percent in the model without home production.
39:1481:86337:24:22:57:550:91:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The difference between the two models reflects predominately the differential cost of dispersion
39:1482:86433:22:12:57:550:69:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:parameters in order to satisfy the government budget constraint.  By contrast, when we calculate optimal taxes
39:1483:86543:12:0:57:385:57:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:(τ0,τ1) in Section4.1.4, we keep constantGto its initial equilibrium value.
40:1484:86620:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in allocations rather than aggregate productivity changesλpwhich are relatively similar across
40:1485:86714:25:25:57:105:689:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f6:models.26
40:1486:86723:25:25:75:551:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The larger dispersion costs of heterogeneity in the home production model reflect the costs of
40:1487:86817:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:dispersion in productivity rather than preferences.  To see this, in the second row we shut down
40:1488:86913:25:24:57:550:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:heterogeneity  in  productivitieszMandzNwhile  we  maintain  heterogeneity  in  the  disutilities
40:1489:87010:24:25:57:550:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of  workBandD.   We  find  even  larger  welfare  effects  than  row  1  and,  thus,  conclude  that
40:1490:87110:25:25:57:551:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:heterogeneity inBandDis not important for the welfare effects of eliminating all heterogeneity.
40:1491:87205:25:25:57:551:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:In  the  third  row,  we  shut  down  only  heterogeneity  in  market  productivityzMand  find  that
40:1492:87305:25:25:57:550:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:eliminating this source of dispersion carries larger welfare gains in the model with home production
40:1493:87405:25:25:57:550:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:than in the model without.  In the fourth row, we shut down heterogeneity in home productivity
40:1494:87499:25:25:57:551:465:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:zNonly.  We find large welfare effects, which illustrates the key role ofzNheterogeneity for the
40:1495:87595:25:31:57:128:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:welfare losses.
40:1496:87610:31:33:57:254:409:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:4.1.4    Optimal Tax Progressivity
40:1497:87644:33:25:57:550:376:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:This section contrasts the optimal progressivity of the tax system between the model with and
40:1498:87737:25:25:57:550:351:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:without  home  production.   Relative  to  our  previous  inequality  metrics,  the  optimal  taxation
40:1499:87839:25:24:57:550:326:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:exercise  mixes  redistribution  with  efficiency  concerns  because  the  optimal  progressivity  of  the
40:1500:87945:24:25:57:550:302:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:tax  system  increases  with  redistributive  motives  and  decreases  with  the  efficiency  losses  from
40:1501:88051:25:25:57:550:277:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:distorting labor allocations.  However, this exercise allows us to more directly link our inequality
40:1502:88151:25:25:57:137:252:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:result to policy.
40:1503:88168:25:25:75:551:227:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Given government expendituresGfixed at its initial equilibrium level, the government chooses
40:1504:88260:25:37:57:406:202:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:tax function parametersτ≡(τ0,τ1) to maximize utilitarian welfare:
40:1505:88325:37:7:65:87:165:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:max
40:1506:88328:7:0:74:78:158:9:g_d0_f11:9:g_d0_f11:τ
40:1507:88329:0:28:92:99:182:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f12:∫
40:1508:88330:28:0:99:102:154:9:g_d0_f11:9:g_d0_f11:ι
40:1509:88331:0:27:105:551:165:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:U(c(τ),h(τ);ι)dΦ(ι),(21)
40:1510:88355:27:15:67:551:138:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:26The  welfare  effects  in  Table6  reflect  heterogeneity  both  within  age  and  over  the  life-cycle  because  each
40:1511:88476:15:12:57:550:123:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:counterfactual imposes a constant value of the source of heterogeneity for households of all ages.  We have repeated
40:1512:88592:12:12:57:550:111:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:these exercises by shutting down only the within-age heterogeneity and allowing each source of heterogeneity to
40:1513:88703:12:12:57:550:99:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:take its mean value over the life-cycle as shown previously in Figure1.  Appendix TableA.8shows similar welfare
40:1514:88814:12:12:57:550:87:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:effects to those shown in Table6 and,  therefore,  we conclude that the welfare effects predominately reflect the
40:1515:88927:12:0:57:228:75:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:within-age component of heterogeneity.

42:1555:91312:32:32:57:487:436:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:4.2    The Role of Productivity and Preference Heterogeneity
42:1556:91372:32:25:57:550:404:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Using four different metrics of inequality, we have demonstrated that home production amplifies
42:1557:91467:25:25:57:550:379:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:inequality across households.  In this section we explore the roles of productivity heterogeneity
42:1558:91564:25:25:57:550:354:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and preference heterogeneity at home in amplifying inequality across households. We compare our
42:1559:91659:25:25:57:550:329:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:baseline model with both productivity and preference heterogeneity to two nested versions of the
42:1560:91755:25:25:57:550:304:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:home production model.  In the first version, we set the preference weightωP= 0 and, therefore,
42:1561:91850:25:25:57:550:279:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:differences in the allocation of time across households originate from productivity.  In the second
42:1562:91949:25:25:57:551:254:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:version, we set the preference weightωN= 0 and, therefore, differences in the allocation of time
42:1563:92045:25:24:57:287:229:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:across households originate from preferences.
42:1564:92090:24:25:75:551:205:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Table7 summarizes our results. The first column presents the four inequality metrics (averaged
42:1565:92184:25:25:57:550:180:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:across all ages) in the model without home production and the last three columns present the
42:1566:92276:25:25:57:550:155:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:metrics in the three versions of the home production model.  In the home production model with
42:1567:92370:25:25:57:550:130:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:only productivity heterogeneity, all inequality metrics are magnified relative to the baseline with
42:1568:92469:25:25:57:550:105:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:both productivity and preference heterogeneity.  If there was only preference heterogeneity, there
42:1569:92567:25:25:57:550:80:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:would be no significant difference in inequality between the model with and the model without
42:1570:92660:25:0:57:550:55:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:home production.  We conclude that productivity heterogeneity in the home sector rather than
43:1571:92754:0:40:57:471:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:preference heterogeneity is important in amplifying inequality across households.
43:1572:92835:40:37:57:256:674:17:g_d0_f4:17:g_d0_f4:5    Sensitivity Analyses

43:1574:92964:25:24:57:550:612:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:to  the  parameterization,  the  measures  of  consumption,  the  subsamples  of  the  population,  and
43:1575:93067:24:25:57:551:588:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:measurement  error  in  observables.   Each  row  in  Table8  corresponds  to  a  different  sensitivity
43:1576:93171:25:25:57:550:563:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:analysis.  For both models, the columns show the standard deviation in equivalent variationT,

44:1622:96220:29:14:57:550:189:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:Table 8 presents each sensitivity analysis in a row.  Columns show the four inequality metrics for the model without
44:1623:96336:14:35:57:389:175:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:home production (ωM= 1) and the model with home production (ωM<1).
44:1624:96402:35:24:57:550:140:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:φ.  The main difference with our baseline arises in terms of the optimal progressivity which is
44:1625:96497:24:25:57:550:116:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:significantly affected by the value ofφ.  Because a higher value ofφincreases the efficiency losses
44:1626:96596:25:25:57:550:91:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:from  a  progressive  tax  system,  we  obtain  a  lowerτ1in  the  model  with  home  production  and
44:1627:96697:25:0:57:326:66:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:φ= 20 than in the model without home production.
45:1628:96747:0:25:75:551:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:In  rows  10  and  11  we  show  that  our  results  are  robust  under  two  alternative  measures  of
45:1629:96850:25:25:57:551:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:market expenditurescM.  In row 10 we use food only whereas in row 11 we use all expenditures
45:1630:96942:25:25:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:including health, education, and durables.  The inequality metrics and the gap between the two
45:1631:97036:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:models are generally similar to the baseline which used nondurable consumption excluding health
45:1632:97131:25:24:57:551:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and education.  From the four metrics, the optimal progressivityτ1is the most sensitive to the
45:1633:97225:24:25:57:184:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:measure of consumption.
45:1634:97248:25:25:75:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:In  rows  12  to  19  of  Table8  we  repeat  our  analyses  in  subsamples  of  households  defined
45:1635:97348:25:25:57:550:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:along their marital status, employment status of the spouse of the head, number of children, and
45:1636:97444:25:25:57:550:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:education. Repeating our analyses for different samples allows us to explore whether our inequality
45:1637:97543:25:25:57:550:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:results  reflect  within  group  inequality  or  inequality  across  groups.   Additionally,  verifying  our
45:1638:97651:25:25:57:550:465:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:results at the subgroup level is reassuring because one would expect dimensions of heterogeneity
45:1639:97747:25:25:57:550:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:that we did not model, such as spousal employment at the extensive margin or number of children,
45:1640:97843:25:25:57:356:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:to be less important within more narrowly defined groups.
45:1641:97900:25:25:75:551:390:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Our  results  are  remarkably  stable  at  the  subgroup  level,  with  the  home  production  model
45:1642:98000:25:25:57:550:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:always generating more inequality than the model without home production according to all four
45:1643:98094:25:24:57:550:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:metrics.   Row  12  shows  the  sample  of  singles,  for  which  the  inequality  gap  between  models  is
45:1644:98201:24:25:57:550:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:generally smaller.  Rows 13 and 14 show subsamples of married households according to whether
45:1645:98294:25:25:57:550:291:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the spouse is working or not. Reassuringly for the mechanisms we have stressed, we obtain a larger
45:1646:98392:25:25:57:550:266:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:inequality gap for the group of non-working spouses for which we would expect home productivity
45:1647:98487:25:25:57:550:241:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:differences to be more important.  In rows 15 to 17 we differentiate according to the number of
45:1648:98582:25:25:57:550:216:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:children present in the household.  We obtain larger inequality gaps among households with more
45:1649:98677:25:25:57:550:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:children, which highlights the importance of time spent on child care for our results.  Finally, rows
45:1650:98778:25:25:57:550:166:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:18 and 19 show results for married households with a head who has not completed college and
45:1651:98869:25:25:57:550:141:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:with a head who has completed college or more.  Our results are similar to the baseline with the
45:1652:98965:25:25:57:550:116:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:exception of the optimal progressivityτ1which declines substantially in the model without home
45:1653:99059:25:25:57:116:91:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:production.

46:1671:99913:29:14:57:550:362:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:Table 9 presents each sensitivity analysis in a row.  Columns show the four inequality metrics for the model without
46:1672:100029:14:33:57:389:348:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:home production (ωM= 1) and the model with home production (ωM<1).
46:1673:100095:33:25:57:550:315:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:baseline case without measurement error.  For the other rows, we consider a classical measurement
46:1674:100192:25:33:57:435:290:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:error model in which the reported value of a variablexfor householdιis:
46:1675:100263:33:33:65:551:257:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:logx(ι) = logx∗(ι) +m(ι),(23)
46:1676:100292:33:25:57:550:224:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:wherex∗is the true and unobserved value of variablexandmdenotes a classical measurement
46:1677:100379:25:25:57:550:199:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:error with varianceσ2m.  Rows 2 to 4 show results with measurement error in market consumption
46:1678:100473:25:25:57:550:174:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:(x=cM), rows 5 to 7 show results with measurement error in market hours (x=hM), and rows
46:1679:100561:25:25:57:551:149:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:8 to 10 show results with measurement error in home hours (x={hN,hP}).  For each variable
46:1680:100650:25:25:57:550:124:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:we show measurement errors that absorb 20, 50, and 80 percent of the variance of the observed
46:1681:100743:25:25:57:100:99:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:variable.
46:1682:100752:25:25:75:550:74:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Our process is to draw measurement error with varianceσ2macross households for each variable
46:1683:100844:25:0:57:551:49:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and then use the true valuesx∗from equation (23) as the data for the extraction of the sources
47:1684:100940:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of heterogeneity (α,ε,B,D,zN).  The table shows the calculation of the four inequality metrics
47:1685:101034:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:for  the  same  parameters  as  in  our  baseline  case  without  measurement  error.   We  find  small
47:1686:101137:25:25:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:differences relative to our baseline results.  Inequality tends to decline with measurement error in
47:1687:101237:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:consumption, but not differentially across the two models.  For market hours, measurement error
47:1688:101332:25:24:57:551:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:affects only the optimal progressivityτ1, but we always find that progressivity is higher in the
47:1689:101428:24:25:57:550:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:home production model.  Finally, most of our results are robust to measurement error of up to
47:1690:101521:25:25:57:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:80 percent of the variance of home hours.  At that level,  the dispersion in equivalent variation
47:1691:101618:25:25:57:550:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in  the  model  with  home  production  is  equalized  to  the  dispersion  in  the  model  without  home
47:1692:101723:25:25:57:550:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:production.  We still obtain higher inequality with home production using the other three metrics
47:1693:101820:25:40:57:123:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:of inequality.
47:1694:101834:40:37:57:341:450:17:g_d0_f4:17:g_d0_f4:6    Other Datasets and Countries
47:1695:101867:37:25:57:550:413:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We show the similarity of the inequality results between the CEX/ATUS and three alternative
47:1696:101958:25:25:57:550:388:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:datasets, the Panel Study of Income Dynamics (PSID), the Japanese Panel Survey of Consumers
47:1697:102049:25:24:57:550:363:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:(JPSC),  and  the  Longitudinal  Internet  Studies  for  the  Social  Sciences  from  the  Netherlands
47:1698:102151:24:35:57:94:339:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:(LISS).
47:1699:102158:35:33:57:407:304:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:6.1    Comparison between CEX/ATUS and PSID
47:1700:102201:33:25:57:550:271:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The PSID has two advantages relative to the CEX/ATUS. It has a panel dimension and contains
47:1701:102292:25:25:57:550:246:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:information on both expenditures and time spent on home production.  However, we prefer using
47:1702:102385:25:25:57:550:221:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the  CEX/ATUS  sample  for  our  baseline  analyses  for  three  reasons.   First,  the  PSID  survey
47:1703:102486:25:24:57:550:196:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:question covers aggregated time spent on home production, which does not allow us to separate
47:1704:102579:24:25:57:551:172:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:credibly  home  hourshNin  the  sector  with productivity heterogeneity  from  home hourshPin
47:1705:102672:25:25:57:550:147:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the sector with preference heterogeneity.  Second, the PSID has lower quality of time use data as
47:1706:102769:25:25:57:550:122:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:compared to the time diaries from the ATUS. In particular, it is not clear if respondents include
47:1707:102866:25:18:57:551:97:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:activities  such  as  child  care  and  shopping  in  their  reported  home  hours.27Third,  food  is  the
47:1708:102972:18:16:67:550:79:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:27The survey question is “About how much time do you spend on housework in an average week?  I mean time
47:1709:103076:16:0:57:344:63:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:spent cooking, cleaning, and doing other work around the house.”
48:1710:103142:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:only measure of consumption which is consistently covered across surveys.  Later surveys cover
48:1711:103236:25:25:57:536:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:expanded categories but the sample size is significantly smaller than the CEX/ATUS sample.
48:1712:103326:25:25:75:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We use two versions of the PSID. In the version in whichcMincludes only expenditures on food,
48:1713:103419:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:we have 69,951 observations between 1975 and 2014 for 10,992 households.  In the version in which
48:1714:103516:25:24:57:551:614:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:cMincludes  food,  utilities,  child  care  expenses,  clothing,  home  insurance,  telecommunication,
48:1715:103618:24:25:57:550:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:transportation,  and  home  repairs,  we  have  13,626  observations  between  2004  and  2014.   PSID
48:1716:103720:25:25:57:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:does not have information that allows us to disaggregate time spent on home production between
48:1717:103814:25:25:57:551:540:12:g_d0_f9:12:g_d0_f5:hNandhP.  To make the analyses as comparable as possible to CEX/ATUS, we consider three
48:1718:103901:25:25:57:550:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:cases.  The first is when all home hours belong tohNin the sector with productivity heterogeneity.
48:1719:103999:25:25:57:550:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The second case, which is more similar to our benchmark in the CEX/ATUS, is that home hours
48:1720:104090:25:25:57:549:465:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f11:are split equally between the two sectors.28The third case is when all home hours belong tohP
48:1721:104183:25:25:57:278:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:in the sector with preference heterogeneity.
48:1722:104227:25:25:75:550:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Table10reassesses our conclusions regarding inequality.29The first panel repeats the find-
48:1723:104317:25:25:57:551:390:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:ings  of  Table7  in  the  CEX/ATUS  for  the  four  inequality  metrics  in  the  model  without  home
48:1724:104420:25:25:57:550:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:production, the home production model with only productivity heterogeneity, the baseline home
48:1725:104513:25:24:57:550:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:production  model  with  both  productivity  and  preference  heterogeneity,  and  the  home  produc-
48:1726:104614:24:25:57:550:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:tion model with only preference heterogeneity.  The second panel reports these statistics for the
48:1727:104711:25:25:57:550:291:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:version  of  the  PSID  that  includes  an  expanded  set  of  consumption  categories.   The  third  and
48:1728:104816:25:25:57:550:266:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:fourth panels report these statistics for the CEX/ATUS and PSID datasets when we restrict our
48:1729:104909:25:25:57:291:241:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:measure of consumption to include only food.
48:1730:104953:25:25:75:551:216:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Our  conclusions  regarding  inequality  and  the  role  of  productivity  heterogeneity  are  stable
48:1731:105054:25:25:57:550:191:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:across the four datasets.  First,  the baseline model with home production generates higher in-
48:1732:105149:25:25:57:550:166:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:equality than the model without home production.  Second, in the model with only productivity
48:1733:105242:25:18:57:550:141:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:heterogeneity, all inequality metrics are magnified relative to the baseline with both productivity
48:1734:105341:18:12:67:529:123:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:28Differences still exist, however, becausehNandhPin the CEX/ATUS are far from perfectly correlated.
48:1735:105441:12:15:67:550:111:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:29To isolate differences in the samples rather than differences stemming from parameters, we keep parameters
48:1736:105549:15:12:57:551:96:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:fixed at their values shown in Table5.  We follow a similar strategy with the JPSC and the LISS datasets later.  The
48:1737:105665:12:12:57:551:84:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:exception is the preference weights (ωM,ωN,ωP) that we recalibrate to hit the same targets as in the CEX/ATUS
48:1738:105774:12:0:57:148:72:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:using equations (13).

49:1764:106534:29:15:57:550:165:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:Table 10 shows the four inequality metrics for the model without home production, the home production model
49:1765:106641:15:14:57:550:150:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:with only productivity heterogeneity, the baseline home production model with both productivity and preference
49:1766:106751:14:15:57:550:136:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:heterogeneity, and the home production model with only preference heterogeneity.  Parametersτ0,τ1, andφare
49:1767:106857:15:14:57:551:121:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:held constant to their values shown in Table5.  For each column, the values forηare given by 0.90, 0.53, 0.50, and
49:1768:106971:14:14:57:550:107:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:0.57 (constant across panels).  The preference weights (ωM,ωN,ωP) for the home production models are given by
49:1769:107080:14:15:57:550:93:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:(0.45,0.55,0),  (0.40,0.38,0.22),  (0.54,0,0.46) in the first panel;  (0.51,0.49,0),  (0.46,0.32,0.22),  (0.60,0,0.40) in
49:1770:107201:15:14:57:551:78:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:the second panel; (0.44,0.56,0), (0.39,0.38,0.22), (0.54,0,0.46) in the third panel; (0.49,0.51,0), (0.43,0.33,0.24),
49:1771:107318:14:0:57:202:64:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:(0.57,0,0.43) in the fourth panel.
50:1772:107354:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and preference heterogeneity.  Third, if there was only preference heterogeneity, there would be
50:1773:107450:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:no significant difference in inequality between the model with and the model without home pro-
50:1774:107544:25:25:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:duction.  The only significant change in the PSID relative to the CEX/ATUS is in the optimal
50:1775:107636:25:25:57:456:639:12:g_d0_f5:9:g_d0_f6:progressivityτ1which displays a smaller difference between the two models.30
50:1776:107712:25:24:75:551:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Our results using the PSID are particularly reassuring because we do not take a stance about
50:1777:107804:24:25:57:551:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the classification of time uses betweenhNandhP.  Therefore, the result that inequality is higher
50:1778:107900:25:25:57:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:with home production does not hinge on which activities are subject to productivity heterogeneity
50:1779:107997:25:25:57:550:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and which activities are subject to preference heterogeneity.  What is important for this result is
50:1780:108096:25:35:57:488:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:that some portion of home production time is subject to productivity heterogeneity.
50:1781:108179:35:32:57:475:480:14:g_d0_f4:14:g_d0_f4:6.2    Comparison between US, Japan, and the Netherlands
50:1782:108235:32:25:57:550:448:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:In this section, we repeat our analyses to datasets from other countries.  As in the PSID, these
50:1783:108331:25:25:57:550:423:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:datasets have in general limited information that allows us to disaggregate time spent on home
50:1784:108425:25:25:57:551:398:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:production betweenhNandhP.  To make the analyses as comparable as possible to CEX/ATUS,
50:1785:108512:25:25:57:551:373:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:we consider the three cases of all home hours belonging tohNin the sector with productivity
50:1786:108603:25:25:57:550:348:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:heterogeneity, of splitting home hours equally between the two sectors, and of all home hours be-
50:1787:108700:25:25:57:551:323:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:longing tohPin the sector with preference heterogeneity. We apply the same sampling restrictions
50:1788:108796:25:25:57:410:298:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:as in the CEX/ATUS and focus our analyses on married households.
50:1789:108860:25:25:75:551:273:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The first dataset is the Japanese Panel Survey of Consumers (JPSC; see, for example, Lise
50:1790:108949:25:25:57:551:248:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and  Yamada,2018).   The  JPSC  records  information  for  time  spent  on  commuting,  working,
50:1791:109045:25:25:57:550:223:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:studying, home production and child care, leisure, and sleeping, personal care and eating.  For
50:1792:109140:25:24:57:550:198:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:home  hours  we  use  the  variable  for  home  production  and  child  care  and  for  market  hours  we
50:1793:109245:24:25:57:550:174:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:use the hours worked.  To calculate the home and market hours for a given week, we weight the
50:1794:109338:25:18:57:550:149:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:time use on workdays and days off by the number of days worked.  Our measure of consumption
50:1795:109429:18:16:67:551:131:7:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f8:30Appendix FiguresA.3andA.4display the age profiles for the means and variances of the sources of hetero-
50:1796:109534:16:12:57:550:115:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:geneity (α,ε,B,D,logzN) from the version of the PSID with food in the baseline case which splits home hours
50:1797:109641:12:12:57:550:103:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:equally betweenhNandhP.  The difference relative to the means and variances we extracted using the CEX/ATUS
50:1798:109748:12:12:57:550:91:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:is that we obtain these age profiles by regressing each inferred shifter on age and year dummies and an individual
50:1799:109862:12:12:57:550:79:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:fixed effect.  Therefore, these profiles reflect the within-household evolution of the sources of heterogeneity.  Despite
50:1800:109983:12:0:57:547:67:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f8:this difference, most of age profiles in the PSID are quantitatively similar to the age profiles in the CEX/ATUS.
51:1801:110098:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:expenditures includes food, utilities, apparel, transport, culture and leisure, communication, trips
51:1802:110198:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and activities, house and land rent.  Our findings are robust to broader and narrower definitions of
51:1803:110298:25:25:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:consumption expenditures. Our final dataset has 12,423 observations between 1998 and 2014. The
51:1804:110392:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:second dataset is the Longitudinal Internet Studies for the Social Sciences from the Netherlands
51:1805:110488:25:24:57:551:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:(LISS;  see,  for  example,Cherchye,  Demuynck,  De  Rock,  and  Vermeulen,2017),  administered
51:1806:110583:24:25:57:550:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:by CentERdata in the Netherlands.  The dataset is based on a representative sample of Dutch
51:1807:110674:25:25:57:550:565:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:households who participate in monthly surveys.  We use the three waves (2009, 2010, and 2012)
51:1808:110767:25:25:57:550:540:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:that contain information on time use.  Home production time includes household chores,  child
51:1809:110860:25:25:57:550:515:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:care, and administrative chores.  Market hours are measured by time spent on paid work, which
51:1810:110953:25:25:57:550:490:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:includes commuting time.  Consumption expenditures include food, utilities, home maintenance,
51:1811:111046:25:25:57:483:465:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:transportation, daycare, and child support.  The final dataset has 978 observations.
51:1812:111130:25:25:75:550:440:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Table11summarizes our results.  The first panel repeats our findings in the CEX/ATUS and
51:1813:111218:25:25:57:550:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the other panels show inequality statistics in the JPSC and the LISS. Our conclusions regarding
51:1814:111313:25:25:57:550:390:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:inequality and the role of productivity heterogeneity are stable in other countries as well.  Namely,
51:1815:111414:25:25:57:550:365:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:the  baseline  model  with  home  production  always  generates  higher  inequality  than  the  model
51:1816:111515:25:24:57:550:340:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:without home production.  All inequality statistics are magnified in the home production model
51:1817:111609:24:25:57:550:316:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:with only productivity heterogeneity, whereas with only preference heterogeneity there would be
51:1818:111704:25:40:57:537:291:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:no significant difference in inequality between the models with and without home production.
51:1819:111796:40:37:57:179:251:17:g_d0_f4:17:g_d0_f4:7    Conclusion
51:1820:111811:37:25:57:550:214:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:The literature examining the causes, welfare consequences, and policy implications of the substan-
51:1821:111909:25:25:57:550:189:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:tial labor market dispersion we observe across households typically abstracts from the possibility
51:1822:112007:25:25:57:550:164:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:that households can produce goods and services outside of the market sector.  We revisit these
51:1823:112101:25:25:57:550:139:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:issues  taking  into  account  that  households  spend  a  significant  amount  of  their  time  in  home
51:1824:112206:25:25:57:550:114:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:production.  Our model incorporates non-separable preferences between expenditures and time
51:1825:112297:25:24:57:550:89:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:and home productivity and preference differences across households into a standard incomplete
51:1826:112390:24:0:57:248:65:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:markets model with uninsurable risk.

52:1853:113753:34:25:75:551:180:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:We reach several substantial conclusions.  We find that home production amplifies welfare-
52:1854:113843:25:25:57:550:155:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:based differences across households and inequality is larger than we thought.  Our result is sur-
52:1855:113940:25:25:57:550:130:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:prising  given  that  a  priori  one  could  expect  that  home  production  tends  to  compress  welfare
52:1856:114045:25:25:57:550:105:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:differences  that  originate  in  the  market  sector  when  households  are  sufficiently  willing  to  sub-
52:1857:114154:25:25:57:550:80:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:stitute between market expenditures and time in the production of home goods.  We show that
52:1858:114245:25:0:57:550:55:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:home productivity is an important source of within-age and life-cycle differences in consumption
53:1859:114343:0:25:57:550:714:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:expenditures  and  time  allocation  across  households.   Through  the  lens  of  the  model,  we  infer
53:1860:114448:25:25:57:550:689:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:that home production does not offset differences that originate in the market sector because pro-
53:1861:114545:25:25:57:550:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:ductivity differences in the home sector are large and the time input in home production does
53:1862:114638:25:25:57:550:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:not covary with consumption expenditures and wages in the cross section of households.  These
53:1863:114731:25:24:57:550:614:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:results  support  the  view  that  the  optimal  tax  system  should  feature  more  progressivity  when
53:1864:114835:24:58:57:467:590:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:incorporating the fact that households can produce goods and services at home.
